<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jeunes_Mari%C3%A9s_dans_le_parc_dAk_Saray_(Shahrisabz)_(6018352949).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Jeunes_Mari%C3%A9s_dans_le_parc_dAk_Saray_%28Shahrisabz%29_%286018352949%29.jpg/220px-Jeunes_Mari%C3%A9s_dans_le_parc_dAk_Saray_%28Shahrisabz%29_%286018352949%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Jeunes_Mari%C3%A9s_dans_le_parc_dAk_Saray_%28Shahrisabz%29_%286018352949%29.jpg/330px-Jeunes_Mari%C3%A9s_dans_le_parc_dAk_Saray_%28Shahrisabz%29_%286018352949%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Jeunes_Mari%C3%A9s_dans_le_parc_dAk_Saray_%28Shahrisabz%29_%286018352949%29.jpg/440px-Jeunes_Mari%C3%A9s_dans_le_parc_dAk_Saray_%28Shahrisabz%29_%286018352949%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jeunes_Mari%C3%A9s_dans_le_parc_dAk_Saray_(Shahrisabz)_(6018352949).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Newlywed" title="Newlywed">Newlywed</a> couples visit <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a>'s statues to receive wedding blessings in <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signing_the_Nikah.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2016" data-file-width="1512" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Signing_the_Nikah.jpg/220px-Signing_the_Nikah.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Signing_the_Nikah.jpg/330px-Signing_the_Nikah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Signing_the_Nikah.jpg/440px-Signing_the_Nikah.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Signing_the_Nikah.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Muslim bride of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> origin signing the <i>nikkah nama</i> or <a href="/wiki/Marriage_certificate" title="Marriage certificate">marriage certificate</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muslim_wedding_in_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="467" data-file-width="699" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Muslim_wedding_in_India.jpg/220px-Muslim_wedding_in_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Muslim_wedding_in_India.jpg/330px-Muslim_wedding_in_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Muslim_wedding_in_India.jpg/440px-Muslim_wedding_in_India.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muslim_wedding_in_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Muslim couple being wed alongside the <a href="/wiki/Tungabhadra_River" title="Tungabhadra River">Tungabhadra River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Hampi" title="Hampi">Hampi</a>, India</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> also commends marriage, with the age of marriage being whenever the individuals feel ready, financially and emotionally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[263]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Islam, <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">polygyny</a> is allowed while <a href="/wiki/Polyandry" title="Polyandry">polyandry</a> is not, with the specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polygamy_in_Islam" title="Polygamy in Islam">limitation</a> that a man can have no more than four legal wives at any one time and an unlimited number of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_slavery#Concubinage" title="Islamic views on slavery">female slaves</a> as <a href="/wiki/Concubinage" title="Concubinage">concubines</a> who may have rights similar wives, with the exception of not being free unless the man has children with them, with the requirement that the man is able and willing to partition his time and wealth equally among the respective wives and concubines (this practice of concubinage, as in Judaism, is not applicable in contemporary times and has been deemed by scholars as invalid due to shifts in views about the role of slavery in the world).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[264]</a></sup>
</p><p>For a Muslim wedding to take place, the bridegroom and the guardian of the bride (<i><a href="/wiki/Wali" title="Wali">wali</a></i>) must both agree on the marriage. Should the guardian disagree on the marriage, it may not legally take place. If the <i>wali</i> of the girl is her father or paternal grandfather, he has the right to force her into marriage even against her proclaimed will, if it is her first marriage. A guardian who is allowed to force the bride into marriage is called <i><a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Islam#Conditions" title="Marriage in Islam">wali mujbir</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[265]</a></sup>
</p><p>From an Islamic (<a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a>) law perspective, the minimum requirements and responsibilities in a Muslim marriage are that the groom provide living expenses (housing, clothing, food, maintenance) to the bride, and in return, the bride's main responsibility is raising children to be proper Muslims. All other rights and responsibilities are to be decided between the husband and wife, and may even be included as stipulations in the marriage contract before the marriage actually takes place, so long as they do not go against the minimum requirements of the marriage.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_marital_jurisprudence" title="Islamic marital jurisprudence">marriage</a> must take place in the presence of at least two reliable witnesses, with the consent of the guardian of the bride and the consent of the groom. Following the marriage, the couple may consummate the marriage. To create an '<a href="/wiki/Urf" title="Urf">urf</a> marriage, it is sufficient that a man and a woman indicate an intention to marry each other and recite the requisite words in front of a suitable Muslim. The wedding party usually follows but can be held days, or months later, whenever the couple and their families want to; however, there can be no concealment of the marriage as it is regarded as public notification due to the requirement of witnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[269]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a>, marriage may take place without the presence of witnesses as is often the case in temporary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikah_mut%E2%80%98ah" title="Nikah mut‘ah">Nikah mut‘ah</a> (prohibited in Sunni Islam), but with the consent of both the bride and the groom. Following the marriage, they may consummate their marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[270]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joodse_bruiloft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-2598.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="13202" data-file-width="14222" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Joodse_bruiloft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-2598.jpeg/220px-Joodse_bruiloft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-2598.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Joodse_bruiloft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-2598.jpeg/330px-Joodse_bruiloft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-2598.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Joodse_bruiloft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-2598.jpeg/440px-Joodse_bruiloft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-2598.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joodse_bruiloft_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-2598.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Jewish wedding, painting by <a href="/wiki/Jozef_Isra%C3%ABls" title="Jozef Israëls">Jozef Israëls</a>, 1903</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:V03p128a01_Ketubah.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1167" data-file-width="881" decoding="async" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/V03p128a01_Ketubah.jpg/220px-V03p128a01_Ketubah.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/V03p128a01_Ketubah.jpg/330px-V03p128a01_Ketubah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/V03p128a01_Ketubah.jpg/440px-V03p128a01_Ketubah.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:V03p128a01_Ketubah.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Ketubah" title="Ketubah">Ketubah</a> in Hebrew, a Jewish marriage-contract outlining the duties of each partner</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jewish_views_on_marriage" title="Jewish views on marriage">Jewish views on marriage</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, marriage is based on the laws of the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> and is a contractual bond between spouses in which the spouses dedicate to be exclusive to one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[271]</a></sup> This contract is called <a href="/wiki/Erusin" title="Erusin">Kiddushin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[272]</a></sup> Though procreation is not the sole purpose, a Jewish marriage is also expected to fulfill the commandment to have children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[273]</a></sup> The main focus centers around the relationship between the spouses. <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalistically</a>, marriage is understood to mean that the spouses are merging into a single soul. This is why a man is considered "incomplete" if he is not married, as his soul is only one part of a larger whole that remains to be unified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> (Christian <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>) describes a number of marriages, including those of <a href="/wiki/Isaac" title="Isaac">Isaac</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[275]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jacob" title="Jacob">Jacob</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[276]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Samson" title="Samson">Samson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[277]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">Polygyny</a>, or men having multiple wives at once, is one of the most common marital arrangements represented in the Hebrew Bible;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JewEncMar_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-JewEncMar-284">[278]</a></sup> another is that of concubinage (<a href="/wiki/Pilegesh" title="Pilegesh">pilegesh</a>) which was often arranged by a man and a woman who generally enjoyed the same rights as a full legal wife (other means of concubinage can be seen in Judges 19-20 where <a href="/wiki/Raptio" title="Raptio">mass marriage by abduction</a> was practiced as a form of punishment on transgressors).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBE_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBE-285">[279]</a></sup> Today <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Ashkenazi Jews</a> are prohibited to take more than one wife because of a ban instituted on this by <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">Gershom ben Judah</a> (Died 1040).
</p><p>Among ancient Hebrews, marriage was a domestic affair and not a religious ceremony; the participation of a priest or rabbi was not required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[280]</a></sup>
</p><p>Betrothal (<i><a href="/wiki/Erusin" title="Erusin">erusin</a></i>), which refers to the time that this binding contract is made, is distinct from marriage itself (<i><a href="/wiki/Jewish_wedding" title="Jewish wedding">nissu'in</a></i>), with the time between these events varying substantially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JewEncMar_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-JewEncMar-284">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CheyneAndBlackMar_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-CheyneAndBlackMar-287">[281]</a></sup>
In biblical times, a wife was regarded as <a href="/wiki/Personal_property" title="Personal property">personal property</a>, belonging to her husband;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JewEncMar_284-2"><a href="#cite_note-JewEncMar-284">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CheyneAndBlackMar_287-1"><a href="#cite_note-CheyneAndBlackMar-287">[281]</a></sup> the descriptions of the Bible suggest that she would be expected to perform tasks such as spinning, sewing, weaving, manufacture of clothing, fetching of water, baking of bread, and <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[282]</a></sup> However, wives were usually looked after with care, and men with more than one wife were expected to ensure that they continue to give the first wife food, clothing, and marital rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[283]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since a wife was regarded as property, her husband was originally free to divorce her for any reason, at any time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CheyneAndBlackMar_287-2"><a href="#cite_note-CheyneAndBlackMar-287">[281]</a></sup> Divorcing a woman against her will was also banned by <a href="/wiki/Gershom_ben_Judah" title="Gershom ben Judah">Gershom ben Judah</a> for <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Ashkenazi Jews</a>. A divorced couple were permitted to get back together, unless the wife had married someone else after her divorce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[284]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Hinduism" title="Marriage in Hinduism">Marriage in Hinduism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hindu_marriage_ceremony_offering.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Hindu_marriage_ceremony_offering.jpg/220px-Hindu_marriage_ceremony_offering.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Hindu_marriage_ceremony_offering.jpg/330px-Hindu_marriage_ceremony_offering.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Hindu_marriage_ceremony_offering.jpg/440px-Hindu_marriage_ceremony_offering.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hindu_marriage_ceremony_offering.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hindu marriage ceremony from a <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> wedding</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bride_Groom_NP.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="2296" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Bride_Groom_NP.JPG/220px-Bride_Groom_NP.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Bride_Groom_NP.JPG/330px-Bride_Groom_NP.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Bride_Groom_NP.JPG/440px-Bride_Groom_NP.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bride_Groom_NP.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Nepali Hindu couple in marriage ceremony</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> sees marriage as a sacred duty that entails both religious and social obligations. Hindu Dharma has prescribed four <a href="/wiki/Puru%E1%B9%A3%C4%81rtha" title="Puruṣārtha">Purusarthas</a>, that is <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">Artha</a> (Wealth), <a href="/wiki/Kama" title="Kama">Kama</a> (Desires) and <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">Moksha</a>. The purpose of the marriage sanskar is to fulfill the Purushartha of ‘Kama’ and then gradually advance towards ‘Moksha’. Old Hindu literature in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> gives many different types of marriages and their categorization ranging from "Gandharva Vivaha" (instant marriage by mutual consent of participants only, without any need for even a single third person as witness) to normal (present day) marriages, to "Rakshasa Vivaha" ("demoniac" marriage, performed by abduction of one participant by the other participant, usually, but not always, with the help of other persons). In the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Arranged marriage in the Indian subcontinent">arranged marriages</a>, the spouse's parents or an older family member choose the partner, are still predominant in comparison with so-called <a href="/wiki/Love_marriage" title="Love marriage">love marriages</a> until nowadays. The <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Widows%27_Remarriage_Act,_1856" title="Hindu Widows' Remarriage Act, 1856">Hindu Widow's Remarriage Act 1856</a> empowers a Hindu widow to remarry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[285]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_view_of_marriage" title="Buddhist view of marriage">Buddhist view of marriage</a></div>
<p>The Buddhist view of marriage considers marriage a secular affair and thus not a <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacrament</a>. Buddhists are expected to follow the civil laws regarding marriage laid out by their respective governments. Gautama Buddha, being a <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">kshatriya</a> was required by Shakyan tradition to pass a series of tests to prove himself as a warrior, before he was allowed to marry.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikhism">Sikhism</span></h3>
<p>In a Sikh marriage, the couple walks around the <i><a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a></i> holy book four times, and a holy man recites from it in the <a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">kirtan</a> style. The ceremony is known as '<a href="/wiki/Anand_Karaj" title="Anand Karaj">Anand Karaj</a>' and represents the holy union of two souls united as one.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wicca">Wicca</span></h3>
<p>Wiccan marriages are commonly known as handfastings and is a celebration held by Wiccans. Handfastings is an ancient Celtic ritual which the hands are tied together to symbolize the binding of two lives. It is commonly used in Wicca and Pagan ceremonies, but it has become more mainstream and comes up in both religious and secular vows and readings. Although handfastings vary for each Wiccan they often involve honoring Wiccan gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedard_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bedard-292">[286]</a></sup> Wicca has a common marriage vow "for as long as love lasts" instead of the traditional Christian "till death do us part". The first Wicca marriage took place in 1960, between Frederic Lamond and his wife, Gillian. Wicca's are not limited to the traditional marriages regardless of gender and how long they are willing to commit themselves to a lifelong relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedard_292-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bedard-292">[286]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sex is considered a pious and sacred activity for the Wiccans. In some traditions sex is ritualized in performing in the form of the Great Rite, whereby a High Priest and High Priestess invoke the God and Goddesses to possess them before performing sexual intercourse with one another. It is to raise magical energy for the use of spell work. It is said that it is used symbolically, using the athame to symbolize the penis and the chalice to symbolize the vagina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[287]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_income">Health and income</span></h2>
<p>Marriages are correlated with better outcomes for the couple and their children, including higher income for men, better health and lower mortality. Part of these effects is due to the fact that those with better expectations get married more often. According to a <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">systematic review</a> on research literature, a significant part of the effect seems to be due to a true <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Causal" title="Causal">causal</a> effect. The reason may be that marriages make particularly men become more future-oriented and take an economic and other responsibility of the family. The studies eliminate the effect of selectivity in numerous ways. However, much of the research is of low quality in this sense. On the other hand, the causal effect might be even higher if money, working skills and parenting practices are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogenous" title="Endogenous">endogenous</a>. Married men have less drug abuse and alcohol use and are more often at home during nights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[288]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marriage_and_health" title="Marriage and health">Marriage and health</a></div>
<p>Marriage, like other close relationships, exerts considerable influence on <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robles2013_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robles2013-295">[289]</a></sup> Married people experience lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morbidity" title="Morbidity">morbidity</a> and mortality across such diverse health threats as <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attacks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KiecoltGlaser2001_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-KiecoltGlaser2001-296">[290]</a></sup> Research on marriage and health is part of the broader study of the benefits of social relationships.
</p><p>Social ties provide people with a sense of identity, purpose, belonging, and support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[291]</a></sup> Simply being married, as well as the quality of one's marriage, have been linked to diverse measures of health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robles2013_295-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robles2013-295">[289]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="rewrite. This tends to &quot;sort of&quot; duplicate the first sentence. I can't move this first because then it would be too obvious! (January 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The health-protective effect of marriage is stronger for men than women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KiecoltGlaser2001_296-1"><a href="#cite_note-KiecoltGlaser2001-296">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wanic2011_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-wanic2011-298">[292]</a></sup> Marital status—the simple fact of being married—confers more health benefits to men than women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KiecoltGlaser2001_296-2"><a href="#cite_note-KiecoltGlaser2001-296">[290]</a></sup>
</p><p>Women's health is more strongly impacted than men's by marital conflict or satisfaction, such that unhappily married women do not enjoy better health relative to their single counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KiecoltGlaser2001_296-3"><a href="#cite_note-KiecoltGlaser2001-296">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wanic2011_298-1"><a href="#cite_note-wanic2011-298">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saxbe2008_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-saxbe2008-299">[293]</a></sup> Most research on marriage and health has focused on heterosexual couples; more work is needed to clarify the health impacts of <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robles2013_295-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robles2013-295">[289]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Divorce_and_annulment">Divorce and annulment</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">Divorce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country" title="Divorce law by country">Divorce law by country</a></div>
<p>In most societies, the death of one of the partners terminates the marriage, and in monogamous societies, this allows the other partner to remarry, though sometimes after a waiting or mourning period.
</p><p>In some societies, a marriage can be <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulled</a>, when an authority declares that a marriage never happened. Jurisdictions often have provisions for <a href="/wiki/Void_marriage" title="Void marriage">void marriages</a> or <a href="/wiki/Voidable_marriage" title="Voidable marriage">voidable marriages</a>.
</p><p>A marriage may also be terminated through <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a>. Countries that have relatively recently legalized divorce are Italy (1970), Portugal (1975), Brazil (1977), Spain (1981), Argentina (1987), Paraguay (1991), Colombia (1991), Ireland (1996), Chile (2004) and Malta (2011). As of 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a> are the only jurisdictions which do not allow divorce (this is currently under discussion in Philippines).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[294]</a></sup> After divorce, one spouse may have to pay <a href="/wiki/Alimony" title="Alimony">alimony</a>. <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country" title="Divorce law by country">Laws concerning divorce</a> and the ease with which a divorce can be obtained vary widely around the world. After a divorce or an annulment, the people concerned are free to remarry (or marry).
</p><p>A statutory right of two married partners to mutually consent to divorce was enacted in western nations in the mid-20th century. In the United States, <a href="/wiki/No-fault_divorce" title="No-fault divorce">no-fault divorce</a> was first enacted in California in 1969 and the final state to legalize it was New York in 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[295]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 45% of marriages in Britain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[296]</a></sup> and, according to a 2009 study, 46% of marriages in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[297]</a></sup> end in divorce.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>The history of marriage is often considered under <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_family" title="History of the family">History of the family</a> or legal history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[298]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_world">Ancient world</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Near_East">Ancient Near East</span></h4>
<p>Many cultures have legends concerning the origins of marriage. The way in which a marriage is conducted and its rules and ramifications have changed over time, as has the institution itself, depending on the culture or demographic of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[299]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first recorded evidence of marriage ceremonies uniting a man and a woman dates back to approximately 2350 BC, in ancient <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[300]</a></sup> Wedding ceremonies, as well as dowry and divorce, can be traced back to Mesopotamia and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[301]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to ancient Hebrew tradition, a wife was seen as being property of high value and was, therefore, usually, carefully looked after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JewEncMar_284-3"><a href="#cite_note-JewEncMar-284">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CheyneAndBlackMar_287-3"><a href="#cite_note-CheyneAndBlackMar-287">[281]</a></sup> Early nomadic communities in the middle east practiced a form of marriage known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beena_marriage" title="Beena marriage">beena</a></i>, in which a wife would own a tent of her own, within which she retains complete independence from her husband;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WRSKinship167_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-WRSKinship167-308">[302]</a></sup> this principle appears to survive in parts of early Israelite society, as some early passages of the Bible appear to portray certain wives as each owning a tent as a personal possession<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WRSKinship167_308-1"><a href="#cite_note-WRSKinship167-308">[302]</a></sup> (specifically, <a href="/wiki/Jael" title="Jael">Jael</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[303]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sarah" title="Sarah">Sarah</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[304]</a></sup> and Jacob's wives<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[305]</a></sup>).
</p><p>The husband, too, is indirectly implied to have some responsibilities to his wife. The <a href="/wiki/Covenant_Code" title="Covenant Code">Covenant Code</a> orders "If he take him another; her food, her clothing, and her duty of marriage, shall he not diminish(or lessen)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[306]</a></sup> If the husband does not provide the first wife with these things, she is to be divorced, without cost to her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[307]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a> interprets this as a requirement for a man to provide food and clothing to, and have sex with, each of his wives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JewEncHusWif_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-JewEncHusWif-314">[308]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, "duty of marriage" is also interpreted as whatever one does as a married couple, which is more than just sexual activity. And the term diminish, which means to lessen, shows the man must treat her as if he was not married to another.
</p><p>As a <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">polygynous</a> society, the Israelites did not have any laws that imposed marital fidelity on men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CheyneAndBlackJeal_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-CheyneAndBlackJeal-315">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JewEncAdu_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-JewEncAdu-316">[310]</a></sup> However, the prophet Malachi states that none should be faithless to the wife of his youth and that God hates divorce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[311]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">Adulterous</a> married women, adulterous betrothed women, and the men who slept with them, however, were subject to the <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">death penalty</a> by <a href="/wiki/Adultery#In_the_Hebrew_Bible" title="Adultery">the biblical laws against adultery</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[312]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[313]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[314]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Priestly_Code" title="Priestly Code">Priestly Code</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Numbers" title="Book of Numbers">Book of Numbers</a>, if a pregnant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[315]</a></sup> woman was suspected of adultery, she was to be subjected to the <a href="/wiki/Ordeal_of_the_bitter_water" title="Ordeal of the bitter water">Ordeal of Bitter Water</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[316]</a></sup> a form of <a href="/wiki/Trial_by_ordeal" title="Trial by ordeal">trial by ordeal</a>, but one that took a miracle to convict. The <a href="/wiki/Literary_prophets" title="Literary prophets">literary prophets</a> indicate that adultery was a frequent occurrence, despite their strong protests against it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[317]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[319]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[320]</a></sup> and these legal strictness's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CheyneAndBlackJeal_315-1"><a href="#cite_note-CheyneAndBlackJeal-315">[309]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Greece_and_Rome">Classical Greece and Rome</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_ancient_Rome" title="Marriage in ancient Rome">Marriage in ancient Rome</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_wedding_customs" title="Ancient Greek wedding customs">Ancient Greek wedding customs</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>, no specific civil ceremony was required for the creation of a heterosexual marriage – only mutual agreement and the fact that the couple must regard each other as husband and wife accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WILLIAMSON_1998_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-WILLIAMSON_1998-327">[321]</a></sup> Men usually married when they were in their 20s and women in their teens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[322]</a></sup> It has been suggested that these ages made sense for the Greeks because men were generally done with military service or financially established by their late 20s, and marrying a teenage girl ensured ample time for her to bear children, as life expectancies were significantly lower.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Married Greek women had few rights in ancient Greek society and were expected to take care of the house and children.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Time was an important factor in Greek marriage. For example, there were superstitions that being married during a <a href="/wiki/Full_moon" title="Full moon">full moon</a> was good luck and Greeks married in the winter in honor of Hera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WILLIAMSON_19982_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-WILLIAMSON_19982-329">[323]</a></sup> Inheritance was more important than feelings: a woman whose father dies without male heirs could be forced to marry her nearest male relative – even if she had to divorce her husband first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-PT-330">[324]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were several types of marriages in ancient Roman society. The traditional ("conventional") form called <i>conventio in manum</i> required a ceremony with witnesses and was also dissolved with a ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-magnusHirschfeldSexology_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-magnusHirschfeldSexology-331">[325]</a></sup> In this type of marriage, a woman lost her family rights of inheritance of her old family and gained them with her new one. She now was subject to the authority of her husband.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[326]</a></sup> There was the free marriage known as <i>sine manu</i>. In this arrangement, the wife remained a member of her original family; she stayed under the authority of her father, kept her family rights of inheritance with her old family and did not gain any with the new family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RomanEmpireMarriage_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-RomanEmpireMarriage-333">[327]</a></sup> The minimum age of marriage for girls was 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[328]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germanic_tribes">Germanic tribes</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seuso_and_his_wife_at_Lake_Balaton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="506" data-file-width="382" decoding="async" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Seuso_and_his_wife_at_Lake_Balaton.jpg/220px-Seuso_and_his_wife_at_Lake_Balaton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Seuso_and_his_wife_at_Lake_Balaton.jpg/330px-Seuso_and_his_wife_at_Lake_Balaton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Seuso_and_his_wife_at_Lake_Balaton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seuso_and_his_wife_at_Lake_Balaton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seuso and his wife</div></div></div>
<p>Among ancient <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> tribes, the bride and groom were roughly the same age and generally older than their Roman counterparts, at least according to <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The youths partake late of the pleasures of love, and hence pass the age of puberty unexhausted: nor are the virgins hurried into marriage; the same maturity, the same full growth is required: the sexes unite equally matched and robust, and the children inherit the vigor of their parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[329]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Where <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> had set the prime of life at 37 years for men and 18 for women, the <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Code" title="Visigothic Code">Visigothic Code of law</a> in the 7th century placed the prime of life at 20 years for both men and women, after which both presumably married. Tacitus states that ancient Germanic brides were on average about 20 and were roughly the same age as their husbands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[330]</a></sup> Tacitus, however, had never visited the German-speaking lands and most of his information on <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a> comes from secondary sources. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> women, like those of other Germanic tribes, are marked as women from the age of 12 and older, based on archaeological finds, implying that the age of marriage coincided with <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[331]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_family" title="History of the family">History of the family</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_intermarriage" title="Royal intermarriage">Royal intermarriage</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brauysegen_im_Bett.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="1129" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Brauysegen_im_Bett.gif/220px-Brauysegen_im_Bett.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Brauysegen_im_Bett.gif/330px-Brauysegen_im_Bett.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Brauysegen_im_Bett.gif/440px-Brauysegen_im_Bett.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brauysegen_im_Bett.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woodcut. How Reymont and Melusina were betrothed / And by the bishop were blessed in their bed on their wedlock. From the <i><a href="/wiki/Melusine" title="Melusine">Melusine</a></i>, 15th century.</div></div></div>
<p>From the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christian</a> era (30 to 325 CE), marriage was thought of as primarily a private matter, with no uniform religious or other ceremony being required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[332]</a></sup> However, bishop <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_of_Antioch" title="Ignatius of Antioch">Ignatius of Antioch</a> writing around 110 to bishop <a href="/wiki/Polycarp" title="Polycarp">Polycarp</a> of Smyrna exhorts, "[I]t becomes both men and women who marry, to form their union with the approval of the bishop, that their marriage may be according to God, and not after their own lust."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[333]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 12th-century Europe, women took the surname of their husbands and starting in the second half of the 16th century parental consent along with the church's consent was required for marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-those_terrible_middle_ages_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-those_terrible_middle_ages-340">[334]</a></sup>
</p><p>With few local exceptions, until 1545, Christian marriages in Europe were by mutual consent, declaration of intention to marry and upon the subsequent physical union of the parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-upennExcerptFromBook_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-upennExcerptFromBook-341">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marriageDotAbout_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-marriageDotAbout-342">[336]</a></sup> The couple would promise verbally to each other that they would be married to each other; the presence of a priest or witnesses was not required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[337]</a></sup> This promise was known as the "verbum." If freely given and made in the present tense (e.g., "I marry you"), it was unquestionably binding;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-upennExcerptFromBook_341-1"><a href="#cite_note-upennExcerptFromBook-341">[335]</a></sup> if made in the future tense ("I will marry you"), it would constitute a <a href="/wiki/Engagement" title="Engagement">betrothal</a>.
</p><p>In 1552 a wedding took place in Zufia, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Navarre</a>, between Diego de Zufia and Mari-Miguel following the custom as it was in the realm since the Middle Ages, but the man denounced the marriage on the grounds that its validity was conditioned to "riding" her ("<i>si te cabalgo, lo cual dixo de bascuence (...) balvin yo baneça aren senar içateko</i>"). The tribunal of the kingdom rejected the husband's claim, validating the wedding, but the husband appealed to the tribunal in <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza" title="Zaragoza">Zaragoza</a>, and this institution annulled the marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matrimonios_a_lo_Navarro_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matrimonios_a_lo_Navarro-344">[338]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Fueros_of_Navarre" title="Fueros of Navarre">Charter of Navarre</a>, the basic union consisted of a civil marriage with no priest required and at least two witnesses, and the contract could be broken using the same formula.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Church in turn lashed out at those who got married twice or thrice in a row while their formers spouses were still alive. In 1563 the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a>, twenty-fourth session, required that a valid marriage must be performed by a priest before two witnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matrimonios_a_lo_Navarro_344-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matrimonios_a_lo_Navarro-344">[338]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the functions of churches from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> was to register marriages, which was not obligatory. There was no state involvement in marriage and personal status, with these issues being adjudicated in <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_court" title="Ecclesiastical court">ecclesiastical courts</a>. During the Middle Ages marriages were arranged, sometimes as early as birth, and these early pledges to marry were often used to ensure treaties between different royal families, nobles, and heirs of fiefdoms. The church resisted these imposed unions, and increased the number of causes for nullification of these arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-those_terrible_middle_ages_340-1"><a href="#cite_note-those_terrible_middle_ages-340">[334]</a></sup> As Christianity spread during the Roman period and the Middle Ages, the idea of free choice in selecting marriage partners increased and spread with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-those_terrible_middle_ages_340-2"><a href="#cite_note-those_terrible_middle_ages-340">[334]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Medieval Western Europe</a>, later marriage and higher rates of definitive <a href="/wiki/Celibacy" title="Celibacy">celibacy</a> (the so-called "European marriage pattern") helped to constrain patriarchy at its most extreme level. For example, <a href="/wiki/England_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="England in the Middle Ages">Medieval England</a> saw marriage age as variable depending on economic circumstances, with couples delaying marriage until the early twenties when times were bad and falling to the late teens after the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, when there were labor shortages;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[339]</a></sup> by appearances, marriage of adolescents was not the norm in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[340]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[341]</a></sup> Where the strong influence of classical <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> cultures (which were not rigidly patriarchal)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[342]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[343]</a></sup> helped to offset the Judaeo-Roman patriarchal influence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[344]</a></sup> in Eastern Europe the tradition of early and universal marriage (often in early <a href="/wiki/Adolescence" title="Adolescence">adolescence</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[345]</a></sup> as well as traditional <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavic</a> patrilocal custom,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[346]</a></sup> led to a greatly inferior status of women at all levels of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[347]</a></sup>
</p><p>The average age of marriage for most of <a href="/wiki/Western_European_marriage_pattern" title="Western European marriage pattern">Northwestern Europe</a> from 1500 to 1800 was around <a href="/wiki/Hajnal_line" title="Hajnal line">25 years of age</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[348]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[349]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated82_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated82-356">[350]</a></sup> as the Church dictated that both parties had to be at least 21 years of age to marry without the consent of their parents, the bride and groom were roughly the same age, with most brides in their early twenties and most grooms two or three years older,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated82_356-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated82-356">[350]</a></sup> and a substantial number of women married for the first time in their thirties and forties, particularly in urban areas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[351]</a></sup> with the average age at first marriage rising and falling as circumstances dictated. In better times, more people could afford to marry earlier and thus fertility rose and conversely marriages were delayed or forgone when times were bad, thus restricting family size;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[352]</a></sup> after the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, the greater availability of profitable jobs allowed more people to marry young and have more children,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[353]</a></sup> but the stabilization of the population in the 16th century meant fewer job opportunities and thus more people delaying marriages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[354]</a></sup>
</p><p>The age of marriage was not absolute, however, as child marriages occurred throughout the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and later, with just some of them including:
</p>
<ul><li>The 1552 CE marriage between John Somerford and Jane Somerford Brereto, at the ages of 3 and 2, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woman-compendium_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-woman-compendium-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the early 1900s, <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Hirschfeld" title="Magnus Hirschfeld">Magnus Hirschfeld</a> surveyed the age of consent in about 50 countries, which he found to often range between 12 and 16. In the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican</a>, the age of consent was 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ascrim-persp_361-0"><a href="#cite_note-ascrim-persp-361">[355]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>As part of the <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, the role of recording marriages and setting the rules for marriage passed to the state, reflecting <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>'s view that marriage was a "worldly thing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[356]</a></sup> By the 17th century, many of the <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> European countries had a state involvement in marriage.
</p><p>In England, under the Anglican Church, marriage by consent and cohabitation was valid until the passage of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marriage_Act_1753" title="Marriage Act 1753">Lord Hardwicke's Act</a> in 1753. This act instituted certain requirements for marriage, including the performance of a religious ceremony observed by witnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WestLaw_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-WestLaw-363">[357]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paolo_Monti_-_Serie_fotografica_-_BEIC_6363689.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1217" data-file-width="1091" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Paolo_Monti_-_Serie_fotografica_-_BEIC_6363689.jpg/220px-Paolo_Monti_-_Serie_fotografica_-_BEIC_6363689.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Paolo_Monti_-_Serie_fotografica_-_BEIC_6363689.jpg/330px-Paolo_Monti_-_Serie_fotografica_-_BEIC_6363689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Paolo_Monti_-_Serie_fotografica_-_BEIC_6363689.jpg/440px-Paolo_Monti_-_Serie_fotografica_-_BEIC_6363689.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paolo_Monti_-_Serie_fotografica_-_BEIC_6363689.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A marriage in 1960 in Italy. Photo by <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Monti" title="Paolo Monti">Paolo Monti</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>As part of the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a>, in 1563 the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> decreed that a <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a> marriage would be recognized only if the marriage ceremony was officiated by a priest with two witnesses. The Council also authorized a <a href="/wiki/Catechism" title="Catechism">Catechism</a>, issued in 1566, which defined marriage as "The conjugal union of man and woman, contracted between two qualified persons, which obliges them to live together throughout life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte_234-2"><a href="#cite_note-Witte-234">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> colleagues reformulated Christian marriage by enacting the Marriage Ordinance of Geneva, which imposed "The dual requirements of state registration and church consecration to constitute marriage"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte_234-3"><a href="#cite_note-Witte-234">[228]</a></sup> for recognition.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>, Lord Hardwicke's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marriage_Act_1753" title="Marriage Act 1753">Marriage Act 1753</a> required a formal ceremony of marriage, thereby curtailing the practice of <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Marriage" title="Fleet Marriage">Fleet Marriage</a>, an irregular or a clandestine marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[358]</a></sup> These were clandestine or irregular marriages performed at Fleet Prison, and at hundreds of other places. From the 1690s until the Marriage Act of 1753 as many as 300,000 clandestine marriages were performed at Fleet Prison alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[359]</a></sup> The Act required a marriage ceremony to be officiated by an Anglican priest in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a> with two witnesses and registration. The Act did not apply to Jewish marriages or those of Quakers, whose marriages continued to be governed by their own customs.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flodmark-Ehrs_Marriage_2016_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="910" data-file-width="1192" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Flodmark-Ehrs_Marriage_2016_crop.jpg/220px-Flodmark-Ehrs_Marriage_2016_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Flodmark-Ehrs_Marriage_2016_crop.jpg/330px-Flodmark-Ehrs_Marriage_2016_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Flodmark-Ehrs_Marriage_2016_crop.jpg/440px-Flodmark-Ehrs_Marriage_2016_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flodmark-Ehrs_Marriage_2016_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newlyweds after a civil ceremony in the tower of <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_City_Hall" title="Stockholm City Hall">Stockholm City Hall</a> in 2016</div></div></div>
<p>In England and Wales, since 1837, civil marriages have been recognized as a legal alternative to church marriages under the <a href="/wiki/Marriage_Act_1836" title="Marriage Act 1836">Marriage Act 1836</a>. In Germany, civil marriages were recognized in 1875. This law permitted a declaration of the marriage before an official clerk of the civil administration, when both spouses affirm their will to marry, to constitute a legally recognized valid and effective marriage, and allowed an optional private clerical marriage ceremony.
</p><p>In contemporary <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English common law</a>, a marriage is a voluntary <a href="/wiki/Contract" title="Contract">contract</a> by a man and a woman, in which by agreement they choose to become husband and wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[360]</a></sup> Edvard Westermarck proposed that "the institution of marriage has probably developed out of a primeval habit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[361]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the late twentieth century, major social changes in Western countries have led to changes in the demographics of marriage, with the age of first marriage increasing, fewer people marrying, and more couples choosing to <a href="/wiki/Cohabitation" title="Cohabitation">cohabit</a> rather than marry. For example, the number of marriages in Europe decreased by 30% from 1975 to 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[362]</a></sup> As of 2000, the average marriage age range was 25–44 years for men and 22–39 years for women.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_marriage" title="Chinese marriage">Chinese marriage</a></div>
<p>The mythological origin of Chinese marriage is a story about <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCwa" title="Nüwa">Nüwa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fu_Xi" title="Fu Xi">Fu Xi</a> who invented proper marriage procedures after becoming married. In ancient Chinese society, people of the same surname are supposed to consult with their <a href="/wiki/Family_tree" title="Family tree">family trees</a> prior to marriage to reduce the potential risk of unintentional incest. Marrying one's maternal relatives was generally not thought of as incest. Families sometimes intermarried from one generation to another. Over time, Chinese people became more geographically mobile. Individuals remained members of their biological families. When a couple died, the husband and the wife were buried separately in the respective clan's graveyard. In a maternal marriage, a male would become a son-in-law who lived in the wife's home.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Marriage_Law" title="New Marriage Law">New Marriage Law</a> of 1950 radically changed Chinese marriage traditions, enforcing <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamy</a>, equality of men and women, and choice in marriage; <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged marriages</a> were the most common type of marriage in China until then. Starting October 2003, it became legal to marry or divorce without authorization from the couple's work units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[363]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="new marriage law or what? (October 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Although people with infectious diseases such as AIDS may now marry, marriage is still illegal for the mentally ill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[364]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-mexico-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-mexico_122-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Same-sex marriage is legally performed and recognized in the states of <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Aguascalientes" title="Same-sex marriage in Aguascalientes">Aguascalientes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Baja_California" title="Same-sex marriage in Baja California">Baja California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Baja_California_Sur" title="Same-sex marriage in Baja California Sur">Baja California Sur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Campeche" title="Same-sex marriage in Campeche">Campeche</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Chiapas" title="Same-sex marriage in Chiapas">Chiapas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Chihuahua" title="Same-sex marriage in Chihuahua">Chihuahua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Coahuila" title="Same-sex marriage in Coahuila">Coahuila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Colima" title="Same-sex marriage in Colima">Colima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Hidalgo" title="Same-sex marriage in Hidalgo">Hidalgo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Jalisco" title="Same-sex marriage in Jalisco">Jalisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Michoac%C3%A1n" title="Same-sex marriage in Michoacán">Michoacán</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Morelos" title="Same-sex marriage in Morelos">Morelos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Nayarit" title="Same-sex marriage in Nayarit">Nayarit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Nuevo_Le%C3%B3n" title="Same-sex marriage in Nuevo León">Nuevo León</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Oaxaca" title="Same-sex marriage in Oaxaca">Oaxaca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Puebla" title="Same-sex marriage in Puebla">Puebla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Quintana_Roo" title="Same-sex marriage in Quintana Roo">Quintana Roo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_San_Luis_Potos%C3%AD" title="Same-sex marriage in San Luis Potosí">San Luis Potosí</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Mexico_City" title="Same-sex marriage in Mexico City">Mexico City</a> as well as in some municipalities in <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Guerrero" title="Same-sex marriage in Guerrero">Guerrero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Quer%C3%A9taro" title="Same-sex marriage in Querétaro">Querétaro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Zacatecas" title="Same-sex marriage in Zacatecas">Zacatecas</a>. Marriages entered into in these jurisdictions are fully recognized by law throughout Mexico. In other states, same-sex marriage is available by court injunction (<a href="/wiki/Recurso_de_amparo" title="Recurso de amparo">amparo</a>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-netherlands-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-netherlands_123-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Same-sex marriage is performed and recognized by law in <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_the_Netherlands" title="Same-sex marriage in the Netherlands">the Netherlands proper</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Bonaire,_Sint_Eustatius_and_Saba" title="Same-sex marriage in Bonaire, Sint Eustatius and Saba">Bonaire, Sint Eustatius and Saba</a>. Marriages entered into there have minimal recognition in <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Aruba,_Cura%C3%A7ao_and_Sint_Maarten" title="Same-sex marriage in Aruba, Curaçao and Sint Maarten">Aruba, Curaçao and Sint Maarten</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-nz-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-nz_124-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Same-sex marriage is performed and recognized by law in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand proper</a>, but not in <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Tokelau" title="LGBT rights in Tokelau">Tokelau</a>, the <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_the_Cook_Islands" title="LGBT rights in the Cook Islands">Cook Islands</a> or <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Niue" title="LGBT rights in Niue">Niue</a>, which together make up the <a href="/wiki/Realm_of_New_Zealand" title="Realm of New Zealand">Realm of New Zealand</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-uk-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-uk_125-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Except the British Overseas Territories of <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Anguilla" title="LGBT rights in Anguilla">Anguilla</a>, the <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_the_British_Virgin_Islands" title="LGBT rights in the British Virgin Islands">British Virgin Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_the_Cayman_Islands" title="LGBT rights in the Cayman Islands">Cayman Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Montserrat" title="LGBT rights in Montserrat">Montserrat</a> and the <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_the_Turks_and_Caicos_Islands" title="LGBT rights in the Turks and Caicos Islands">Turks and Caicos Islands</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-usa-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-usa_126-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Same-sex marriage is performed and recognized by law in all <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_law_in_the_United_States_by_state" title="Same-sex marriage law in the United States by state">fifty states</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Same-sex marriage in the District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a>, all territories except <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_American_Samoa" title="LGBT rights in American Samoa">American Samoa</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_tribal_nations_in_the_United_States" title="Same-sex marriage in tribal nations in the United States">some tribal nations</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-IACHR-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-IACHR_127-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The IACHR ruling was issued on 9 January 2018, with <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Costa_Rica" title="Same-sex marriage in Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> accepting the result in a national ruling by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Costa_Rica" title="Supreme Court of Costa Rica">Supreme Court of Costa Rica</a> on 8 August 2018. <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_Ecuador" title="Same-sex marriage in Ecuador">Ecuador</a> became the first country in which the international ruling was implemented, following a national ruling by the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Court_of_Ecuador" title="Constitutional Court of Ecuador">Constitutional Court of Ecuador</a> on 12 June 2019.<br/> The other countries that are signatories to the <a href="/wiki/American_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="American Convention on Human Rights">American Convention on Human Rights</a> and recognize the binding jurisdiction of the court, and which do not already have same-sex marriage nationally, are <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Barbados" title="LGBT rights in Barbados">Barbados</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Bolivia" title="LGBT rights in Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recognition_of_same-sex_unions_in_Chile" title="Recognition of same-sex unions in Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_the_Dominican_Republic" title="LGBT rights in the Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recognition_of_same-sex_unions_in_El_Salvador" title="Recognition of same-sex unions in El Salvador">El Salvador</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Guatemala" title="LGBT rights in Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Haiti" title="LGBT rights in Haiti">Haiti</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Honduras" title="LGBT rights in Honduras">Honduras</a>, Mexico, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Nicaragua" title="LGBT rights in Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Panama" title="LGBT rights in Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Paraguay" title="LGBT rights in Paraguay">Paraguay</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Peru" title="LGBT rights in Peru">Peru</a> and <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Suriname" title="LGBT rights in Suriname">Suriname</a>.<br/> <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Dominica" title="LGBT rights in Dominica">Dominica</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Grenada" title="LGBT rights in Grenada">Grenada</a> and <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Jamaica" title="LGBT rights in Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, which are also signatories to the convention, have not agreed to the court's blanket jurisdiction.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>ubi scelus est id, quod non proficit scire, ubi venus mutatur in alteram formam, ubi amor quaeritur nec videtur, iubemus insurgere leges, armari iura gladio ultore, ut exquisitis poenis subdantur infames, qui sunt vel qui futuri sunt rei.</i><a class="external text" href="http://ancientrome.ru/ius/library/codex/theod/liber09.htm#7" rel="nofollow">Ancientrome.ru</a> "where that crime is found, which is unfit even to know, we command the law to arise armed with an avenging sword that the infamous men who are, or shall in future be guilty of it, may undergo the most severe punishments." translation by Lord <a href="/wiki/William_Blackstone" title="William Blackstone">William Blackstone</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Commentaries_on_the_Laws_of_England" title="Commentaries on the Laws of England">Commentaries on the Laws of England</a></i> Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1769, Vol. IV, pp. 215–16.</span>

<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/legis/cth/consol_act/itaa1997240/s995.1.html" rel="nofollow">Income Tax Assessment Act 1997 – Sect 995.1(1)</a>: ""spouse" of an individual includes: (a) another individual (whether of the same sex or a different sex) with whom the individual is in a relationship that is registered under a * State law or * Territory law prescribed for the purposes of section 22B of the Acts Interpretation Act 1901 as a kind of relationship prescribed for the purposes of that section; and (b) another individual who, although not legally married to the individual, lives with the individual on a genuine domestic basis in a relationship as a couple."</span>

<li id="cite_note-190"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-190">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">Domestic violence</a> is today illegal in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_World" title="Western World">Western World</a>, but this is not the case in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, for instance, part of article 340 of the Penal Code states that "<i>he who discovers his wife or one of his female relatives committing adultery and kills, wounds, or injures one of them, is exempted from any penalty</i>. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFAltstein,_HowardSimon,_Rita_James2003">Altstein, Howard &amp; Simon, Rita James (2003). <i>Global perspectives on social issues: marriage and divorce</i>. Lexington, Mass: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lexington_Books" title="Lexington Books">Lexington Books</a>. p. 11. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7391-0588-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7391-0588-7"><bdi>978-0-7391-0588-7</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Global+perspectives+on+social+issues%3A+marriage+and+divorce&amp;rft.place=Lexington%2C+Mass&amp;rft.pages=11&amp;rft.pub=Lexington+Books&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7391-0588-7&amp;rft.au=Altstein%2C+Howard&amp;rft.au=Simon%2C+Rita+James&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMarriage"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-RCA1975-250"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-RCA1975_250-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.rca.org/synod/statements/" rel="nofollow">"Statements of General Synod"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Church_in_America" title="Reformed Church in America">Reformed Church in America</a>. 1975<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">4 June</span> 2021</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Statements+of+General+Synod&amp;rft.pub=Reformed+Church+in+America&amp;rft.date=1975&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.rca.org%2Fsynod%2Fstatements%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMarriage"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-EMCC2017-251"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-EMCC2017_251-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1"><i>Evangelical Methodist Church Discipline</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Methodist_Church_Conference" title="Evangelical Methodist Church Conference">Evangelical Methodist Church Conference</a>. 15 July 2017. pp. 22–21. <q>The marriage contract is so sacred that we advise against seeking divorce on any grounds whatseover. Should any member seek divorce on any unscriptural grounds (Matt. 5:32 "But I say unto you, that whosoever shall put away his wife, saving for the cause of fornication, causeth her to commit adultery; and whosoever shall marry her that is divorced comitteth adultery."), and that well proven, he shall be summoned to appear at a meeting in the local church, with the general board working in co-operation with the local church board. If proven guilty of such offense, he shall be dismissed at once, and no longer considered a member of Evangelical Methodist Church. We advise against the remarriage of all divorced persons, as the scriptures declare in Romans 7:3a "...So then if, while her husband liveth, she be married to another man, she shall be called an adulteress." If any person becoming converted, and having such marital complications as mentioned above in the days of their sin and ignorance, it is our belief that God will and does forgive them; however, we shall not receive such persons into church membership, but with to extend to them the right hand of fellowship, promising the prayers of God's people. Should any pastor, knowingly or unknowingly, receive such persons that have been divorced and remarried into membership, such membership shall not be valid. Ministers are advised to have nothing to do with the re-marriage of persons divorced on any grounds. In the event any person is divorced by an unbelieving companion and shall remain in an unmarried state, retaining his or her Christian integrity, he or she shall not be dismissed or barred from church membership.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Evangelical+Methodist+Church+Discipline&amp;rft.pages=22-21&amp;rft.pub=Evangelical+Methodist+Church+Conference&amp;rft.date=2017-07-15&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMarriage"></span></span>
</li>

Christianity Today. Retrieved on 6 April 2013.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Mulhall2013-258"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Mulhall2013_258-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMulhall2013">Mulhall, Daniel S. (18 September 2013). <i>The Ecumenical Christian Dialogues and The Catechism of the Catholic Church</i>. Paulist Press. p. 155. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-61643-809-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-61643-809-8"><bdi>978-1-61643-809-8</bdi></a>. <q>The Protestant reformers of the sixteenth century were unwilling to call marriage a sacrament because they did not regard matrimony as a necessary means of grace for salvation. Though not necessary for salvation certainly marriage is a means of grace, thus, sacramental in character.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Ecumenical+Christian+Dialogues+and+The+Catechism+of+the+Catholic+Church&amp;rft.pages=155&amp;rft.pub=Paulist+Press&amp;rft.date=2013-09-18&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-61643-809-8&amp;rft.aulast=Mulhall&amp;rft.aufirst=Daniel+S.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMarriage"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-CBE-285"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CBE_285-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFPitts2015">Pitts, Chuck (31 October 2015). <a class="external text" href="https://www.cbeinternational.org/resource/article/priscilla-papers-academic-journal/judges-19-paradigm-understanding-and-responding" rel="nofollow">"Judges 19 as a Paradigm for Understanding and Responding to Human Trafficking"</a>. <i>Priscilla Papers: The Academic Journal of Christians for Biblical Equality</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">28 December</span> 2020</span>. <q>Their solution to the loss of Benjamin’s men was to conquer the town of Jabesh-Gilead—killing everyone except four hundred young virgins—and taking their virgins to repopulate Benjamin. Unfortunately, there were not enough virgins in Jabesh-Gilead for all the men of Benjamin, so virgins participating in a ritual celebration at Shiloh were kidnapped and given to the men of Benjamin.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Priscilla+Papers%3A+The+Academic+Journal+of+Christians+for+Biblical+Equality&amp;rft.atitle=Judges+19+as+a+Paradigm+for+Understanding+and+Responding+to+Human+Trafficking&amp;rft.date=2015-10-31&amp;rft.aulast=Pitts&amp;rft.aufirst=Chuck&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cbeinternational.org%2Fresource%2Farticle%2Fpriscilla-papers-academic-journal%2Fjudges-19-paradigm-understanding-and-responding&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMarriage"></span></span>
